    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11509543126372328828">Aristophanes' comic masterpiece of war and sex remains one of the greatest plays ever written. Led by the title character, the women of the warring city-states of Greece agree to withhold sexual favours with their husbands until they agree to cease fighting. The war of the sexes that ensues makes <i>Lysistrata</i> a bawdy comedy without peer in the history of theatre.</span>

<div id="review_2162010" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2162010" />
    <a title="Seth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/118502-seth"><img alt="Seth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181094355p2/118502.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2162010?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Seth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Seth" href="/user/show/118502-seth">Seth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781420926439" href="/book/show/1583.Lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2162010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3553813659958475411">I hate this book because I got arrested on account of it. I was at the University of Texas' Perry Castaneda Library and it got lost amidst the shuffled stack of books which I dumped into my backpack when I left. <br/><br/>Exiting the library the sensor went off.<br/><br/>Sorry, I forgot to check it out. No big deal, happens all the time. <br/><br/>But the Department of Collegiate Fascism, aka the UTPD, are required to file a report. Bored from arresting 19-year-olds for walking down the street half drunk they show up like it</span>
  <span id="freeText3553813659958475411" style="display:none">I hate this book because I got arrested on account of it. I was at the University of Texas' Perry Castaneda Library and it got lost amidst the shuffled stack of books which I dumped into my backpack when I left. <br /><br />Exiting the library the sensor went off.<br /><br />Sorry, I forgot to check it out. No big deal, happens all the time. <br /><br />But the Department of Collegiate Fascism, aka the UTPD, are required to file a report. Bored from arresting 19-year-olds for walking down the street half drunk they show up like it's the scene of a hostage crisis. And, unlucky me, it turns out I have a warrant for an unpaid alcohol-possession ticket. <br /><br />Still, no big deal. I can go down to the station and pay it. Fine. But this is the lobby of a large student library and I am surrounded by my fellow students, all of us dutifully studying on a Tuesday night. As such, might I please just walk out to the police car?<br /><br />GET AGAINST THE WALL MOTHERFUCKER!<br /><br />A.J. COVER ME WHILE I SEARCH THIS SCUMBAG!<br /><br />GOTCHA COVERED BUDDY! [Loudly cocks shotgun]<br /><br />NO WEAPONS ON HIS TORSO! NOTHING UP HIS SPHINCTER! NOTHING TIED TO HIS BALLSACK... BUT WAIT, LET ME SQUEEZE HARDER! <br /><br />[Sound of testes being crunched by human fist]<br /><br />OK THIS PERP'S CLEAN! GIT ME MY CUFFS!<br /><br />[Sound of me being viciously shackled]<br /><br />YOU GIT THAT ARM! HELP ME DRAG 'EM OUT TO THE SQUAD CAR! HOLD ON! HE'S MEEKLY PROTESTING! HAND ME MY BLUDGEON! <br /><br />[Egregious violence]<br /><br />YEAH! GIT SOME! GIT SOME! GIT SOME MOTHERFUCKER! YEAH A.J. KICK 'EM SOME MORE! GIT SOME, BITCH! Ok he ain't movin'. <br /><br />[Sound of manacled body being dragged across library lobby, accompanied by the stunned silence of onlookers]<br /><br />Thus ended my experience with Lysistrata. I never got to the ending, although in later years my various girlfriends, in the manner of Ghandi protesting English oppression of the Indian subcontinent, were wont to use "Lysistratan nonaction" to protest my rampant drinking and proneness to random street violence.<br /><br />Not recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3553813659958475411" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14032741342314437030">How old is the idea of women withholding sex from men to get what they want? Well, apparently as far back as 405 BC, because that's what happens in this hilarious (and bawdy) Greek comedy. In this play it was "en masse"' with the singular purpose of bringing peace between the warring Athenians and Spartans. Did it work? Well, what do you think?</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2817712008" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2817712008" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2817712008?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2817712008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7168561521688439902">Today Alyssa Milano called for a sex strike in response to Georgia's abortion ban, which raises two questions: 1) Alyssa Milano is still a thing?! and 2) Haven't I heard this before? I can help with the second thing. Milano got it from Aristophanes, who in 411 BC invented the sex strike and, for all you know, dildos.<br/><br/><a href='https://twitter.com/Alyssa_Milano/status/1127040792281767936' title='Linked to the tweet' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557620773i/27491574._SX540_.png' alt='alyssa' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>It's 411 BC and Athens is deep in the Peloponnesian War with Sparta and everyone's pretty stressed about it. In Aristophanes' brilliantly simple idea, the women of both sides org</span>
  <span id="freeText7168561521688439902" style="display:none">Today Alyssa Milano called for a sex strike in response to Georgia's abortion ban, which raises two questions: 1) Alyssa Milano is still a thing?! and 2) Haven't I heard this before? I can help with the second thing. Milano got it from Aristophanes, who in 411 BC invented the sex strike and, for all you know, dildos.<br><br><a href="https://twitter.com/Alyssa_Milano/status/1127040792281767936" title="Linked to the tweet" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557620773i/27491574._SX540_.png" alt="alyssa" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>It's 411 BC and Athens is deep in the Peloponnesian War with Sparta and everyone's pretty stressed about it. In Aristophanes' brilliantly simple idea, the women of both sides organize and throw a sex strike. "Stop fighting," they say, "Or no more humping!" The plot is that they say that and then it works. There's some stuff about dildos, for obvious reasons, but that's about all there is to it. Unfortunately it was a made-up story; in real life this didn't happen and Athens lost the war and that was basically the end of the Golden Age of Democracy. But the play is great - easily Aristophanes's best surviving play - funny and filthy and don't forget, the thing about Greek plays is that you can knock one out in an hour and a glass of wine, so why haven't you read this?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1557620974i/27491596._SX540_.jpg" alt="lysistrata-small" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Translation and illustration courtesy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3893031.Valerie_Schrag" title="GR author page" rel="nofollow noopener">Valerie Schrag</a> for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13152111-the-graphic-canon-vol-1" rel="nofollow noopener">The Graphic Canon</a></i><br><br>Alyssa Milano, who played a kid on the 80s TV show <i>Who's The Boss</i> and is now still a thing, <a href="https://www.vice.com/en_us/article/gyxbw3/no-rights-no-sex-the-powerful-history-of-women-going-on-strike" title="Article about several different kinds of wome's strikes, including this kind" rel="nofollow noopener">isn't the first</a> to try the Lysistrata strategy. It's been done in places as diverse as Colombia, Nigeria, and Italy. It comes up in the US every time Republicans do something particularly assheaded (so, daily).<br><br>It's <a href="https://qz.com/958346/history-shows-that-sex-strikes-are-a-surprisingly-effective-strategy-for-political-change/" title="Article about it" rel="nofollow noopener">not an ineffective strategy,</a> and I don't mean to belittle Alyssa Milano, who performed the voice of "Bimini" in 2012's <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hj8j3pO4jx0" title="" rel="nofollow noopener">Beverly Hills Chihuahua 2</a> and is totally still a thing. Georgia's abortion ban is a nightmare and if this is what it takes it's a small price to pay. (That's what she said! Dunked on my own dick!) And the play's not ineffective either. Like all the best ideas, it's extremely simple and it involves dildos.<br><br><b>Translations</b><br>Valerie Schrag, as mentioned above, has a comic version for the Graphic Canon that's super fun but pretty direly abridged.<br>Douglass Parker has one for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91040208" rel="nofollow noopener">this common Aristophanes collection</a> that's fine but it's trying <i>very very hard</i> to be as cool as Aristophanes was and failing. If you've found a great translation, please do let me know.</span>

<div id="review_533829064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/533829064" />
    <a title="Praj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3381634-praj"><img alt="Praj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501486663p2/3381634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/533829064?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Praj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Praj" href="/user/show/3381634-praj">Praj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3381634-praj?shelf=phanes">phanes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer533829064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16013295771209648745">It had been quite awhile since I contemplated over any books let alone penning a critical appraisal on Goodreads. It was tough trying to get words out of the overwhelming emotional vortex; an obstinate ketchup bottle ignoring the need of a fried potato for the tangy goodness. So, when <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1778252-bird-brian' rel='nofollow noopener'>Brian</a> suggested a group reading of Lysistrata, I was a bit apprehensive. A Greek playwright crossing the dreaded course of fallen heroic tragedies; even more remorse to my cerebral coma; not a luxurious indulgence </span>
  <span id="freeText16013295771209648745" style="display:none">It had been quite awhile since I contemplated over any books let alone penning a critical appraisal on Goodreads. It was tough trying to get words out of the overwhelming emotional vortex; an obstinate ketchup bottle ignoring the need of a fried potato for the tangy goodness. So, when <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/1778252-bird-brian" rel="nofollow noopener">Brian</a> suggested a group reading of Lysistrata, I was a bit apprehensive. A Greek playwright crossing the dreaded course of fallen heroic tragedies; even more remorse to my cerebral coma; not a luxurious indulgence at the moment. Lysistrata is a woman’s name; yes it is and sex is the weapon used to hem the broken olive branch.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“To husband or lover, I’ll not open arms. Though love and denial may enlarge his charms. But still at home, ignoring him, I’ll stay. Bountiful, clad in saffron silk all day. If then he seizes me with by dint of force, I’ll give him reason for a long remorse. I’ll never lie and stare up at the ceiling. Nor like a lion on all four go kneeling. If I keep faith then bounteous cups be mine. Do you swear to this? Then I shall immolate the victim thus.”</b>
</i><br /><br /> Holding a pair of olive logs, a vine torch and a small pot of live embers; Lysistrata and her women folk thus embarked on an egalitarian journey within the locked Acropolis citadel; a long awaited unified cry of misplaced wisdom. Neither the pointless sexist blabber from unassailable old men who rather burn the protesters than give a patient ear nor the wailing of desperate husbands and lovers could shake the well rooted fortitude of this rebellious bunch. Peace is what they strive at the cost of their fornication. <i>We pay taxes, manage finesse with domestic budgetary, and give birth to descendants who will render their youth to deathly absurdities in a unproductive war.</i> Abandoned voices yearning to be heard outside the bedroom in the ubiquitous courtyards of masochism. <br /><br /><b>I’m a free woman</b>; screams this slap-stick engaging play. Aristophanes delineated a cohesive front; an equalized gender dais debating the validity of aggressive hostilities. Wars not only annihilate countries but families too. Common sense is a rarity and idiocy the universal daily crow of a proud rooster. Underestimating the weak is the biggest blunder of an astute strategist. And, 'Groupthink' is not just a term coined by a confident Mr. Janis; harried egocentric faulty pronouncements can even corrupt sincerity. Remember the <i>‘Bay of Pigs’??</i> Nevertheless all is not lost and the inbred humor prances around like a spring rabbit. One cannot help but laugh when distressed over the abstinence issue Myrrhine’s husband Cinesias brings their child to convince to come back to a lovely home and a lonely husband. Even after pledging to bringpeace to the land, Myrrhine does not give in to the carnal needs bringing Cinesias to tear his hair out.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>CINESIAS<br />A wicked thing, as I repeat.<br />O Zeus, O Zeus,<br />Canst Thou not suddenly let loose<br />Some twirling hurricane to tear<br />Her flapping up along the air<br />And drop her, when she's whirled around,<br />Here to the ground<br />Neatly impaled upon the stake<br />That's ready upright for her sake</b>
</i><br /><br /> Baudrillard was precise in inferring the power of seduction to be greater than the act itself.<br /><br /><br /><i>"Master the kitchen, master the bedroom and so shall rule your husband".</i> The evergreen thumb rule of triumph of one of my elderly aunt’s long-lasting marriage. In a world devoid of any sex toys or cinematic screenings, sex and food was the ultimate seduction of power. <i>“Buy me the silver or no midnight climaxes!” You want me to clean after you; my closed legs will be your eternal marriage gift!”...</i>. Can sex be really used as a weapon by ladies of all societal strata? Power seekers beware of the fairer sex for they have unfailing artillery!! Is the abstinence of sex capable of stopping mindless male aggression of power? Could Silvio Berlusconi minimize the impact of EU crisis if Ruby had protested the Bunga Bunga? Gaddafi would not have met with such a brutal death for being a scoundrel of a dictator. An excellent point put forth by <u>Brian</u>, about the Iraq War; wonders if the search of the indiscernible WMDs would have stopped if Mrs.Bush along with Mrs. Blair transpired Lysistrata proposal at the White House. The new democratic gesticulation could discipline the wildest of men, Napoleon would have been the best candidate; as the saying goes small men huge “ego”. Aristophanes is undoubtedly a visionary for banishing the discrepancies of gender biases bequeathing the ‘weaker’ sex with a new leash of power and control. No more will the patriarchal societies characterize gender roles and women no longer will be pretty bodies sitting on a vagina. Lysistrata’s protest was not designated to demoralize the validity of manhood, but an outright memo of the rarity of common sense and advocacy of peace over a senseless war fought for decades. The weak can be strong when they stand up for their rights and cannot be easily dismissed by mere ignorance. Not only wars, but numerous crimes against can be stopped with the ongoing strategy. The only fear looms is of how long it will be until the newly acquired democratic forum spits an authoritative fire. But, that is yet a farsighted destination and as of now, peace was ultimately restored and the Greeks merrily celebrated with abundance wine and sex. Wasn't that (sex) the ultimate catch after all?<br /><br /><i>
  <b>LYSISTRATA<br />Earth is delighted now; peace is the voice of earth.<br />Spartans, sort out your wives: Athenians, yours.<br />Let each catch hands with his wife and dance his joy,<br />Dance out his thanks, be grateful in music,<br />And promise reformation with his heels.</b>
</i><br /><br /><br />	<br />	<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16013295771209648745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1143008279" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1143008279" />
    <a title="Liz Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet"><img alt="Liz Janet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574021381p2/5114433.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1143008279?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liz Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liz Janet" href="/user/show/5114433-liz-janet">Liz Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486282251" href="/book/show/440998.Lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1143008279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16873656142516332559"><b>TV Commercial:</b> Does your husband and the men of Athens just want to wage war? Do they ignore your pleas for peace no matter how long the Peloponnesian War has been going on? Tired of your men's stupid decisions in such a trying time? Do you wish to end it? Well women of Athens, you are in luck, we have the solution for you, withhold sex from your husbands and lovers, that will bring them back with their tails between their feet and a signed peace document. <br/><b>Women of Athens:</b> Would that not just cr</span>
  <span id="freeText16873656142516332559" style="display:none"><b>TV Commercial:</b> Does your husband and the men of Athens just want to wage war? Do they ignore your pleas for peace no matter how long the Peloponnesian War has been going on? Tired of your men's stupid decisions in such a trying time? Do you wish to end it? Well women of Athens, you are in luck, we have the solution for you, withhold sex from your husbands and lovers, that will bring them back with their tails between their feet and a signed peace document. <br /><b>Women of Athens:</b> Would that not just create divisions between the sexes and a sort of war between the citizens?<br /><b>TV Commercial: </b>ehhhh, most likely. But, their idiocy must be stopped.<br /><b>Lysistrata:</b> You have got a deal! I am starting now. Women let us meet! My time has come to convince you to withhold sex so we can have peace. This cannot possibly backfire. <br /><br />Now that that is aside, THIS IS NEITHER A FEMINIST PLAY, NOR A PACIFIST ONE. I understand why modern readers might interpret it as such, but at the time it was written,and many years after, it was clear that the view was that women were a nuisance that needed to be protected from themselves. It basically begins with these lines: <br /><i> LYSISTRATA<br /> There are a lot of things about us women<br /> That sadden me, considering how men<br /> See us as rascals.<br />CALONICE<br /> As indeed we are!</i><br /><br />Not the greatest sentence for a supposedly feminist play. <br /><br />Now this is also quite a crude play, maybe even more so than the Bard himself. In it men walk about with erect penises for no other reason that them being denied sex and unable to control erections. We have a woman that sort of teases her lover by saying she will do him and them promptly bringing a bed, a mattress, a pillow, a blanket, some oil, and then ends up running back up to the Acropolis where the women are holding out. Their oath to not have sex tells them not to do a certain sexual pose that apparently was popular back then. <br />I would say this play is a bit offensive to men, not as much as women, but it does dictate that men cannot function unless their penis is inside someone's vagina. That kind of does them a disservice. <br />Here's a few reasons why it is not feminist:<br />-The first lines of the play as written above. <br />-Majority of women, except Lysistrata, are presented as voluptuaries. <br />-A magistrate arrives and starts spouting about how women are hysterical and that men should keep better hold of them. <br />-He proceeds to also claim they have too much of a liking to wine, promiscuous sex, and for some weird reasons, exotic cults. <br />-There are two women with these names: "seed-market-porridge-vegetable-sellers" and "garlic-innkeeping-bread-sellers". <br />-Lysistrata uses a woman for her beauty to distract the men enough to have them sign peace. <br /><br />I did laugh nonetheless, I prefer to read it as satire, it makes the funny and cringe worthy moments all the more fun. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16873656142516332559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2998607007" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2998607007" />
    <a title="Chavelli Sulikowska" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16127160-chavelli-sulikowska"><img alt="Chavelli Sulikowska" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534643154p2/16127160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2998607007?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chavelli Sulikowska" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chavelli Sulikowska" href="/user/show/16127160-chavelli-sulikowska">Chavelli Sulikowska</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2998607007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3754933893096105213"><br/>How do you translate comedy that is more than 25 centuries old? With ease apparently, Lysistrata, first performed way back in 411 BC is just as funny now as it must have been for the ancient Greeks! It was while holidaying in the Peloponnese that it occurred to me to brush up on the ancient Greek classics. A good decision since it really contextualised all the history I was literally walking over every day.<br/><br/>The play commences in 431–404 BC with the Peloponnesian War between Athens and Sparta. Alm</span>
  <span id="freeText3754933893096105213" style="display:none"><br />How do you translate comedy that is more than 25 centuries old? With ease apparently, Lysistrata, first performed way back in 411 BC is just as funny now as it must have been for the ancient Greeks! It was while holidaying in the Peloponnese that it occurred to me to brush up on the ancient Greek classics. A good decision since it really contextualised all the history I was literally walking over every day.<br /><br />The play commences in 431–404 BC with the Peloponnesian War between Athens and Sparta. Almost all the men are engaged in what seems to be an interminable conflict. It is Athenian woman, Lysistrata who decides on an unusual course of action to bring the war to an end and restore peace – she calls on women from all the warring states to refuse their men sex until peace is achieved! As you can imagine, this may have been a measure welcomed by many women, but generally unpopular with the gents…<br /><br />There is much debate between the women leading this movement and the men in power, and the dialogue is very witty and funny as justifications for and arguments against are bantered back and forth. Aristophanes is known primarily as a comedian and the play is certainly amusing, though the underlying message is quite sombre: there are very few solutions to war. In reality the Peloponnesian War has been raging for twenty years by the time the play was first staged, and it still wasn’t over. The powerhouse that had been Athens was effectively reduced to a pile of rubble and Sparta emerged as the new seat of power. Albeit at a massive cost to life. It equally raises pertinent questions about the traditional roles of women in society and highlights gender inequality - the fact that the women know that the most effective tool to curb men's behaviour is through their bodies clearly underlines that not much has changed in terms of gender bias over the centuries!<br /><br />An exceptional (and timely) read for me. The ancient Greeks never cease to amaze me with their skills and knowledge in just about everything. Make sure you read a well translated copy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3754933893096105213" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2625115974" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2625115974" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2625115974?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=plays-theatre">plays-theatre</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2625115974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2734548742452058562">Looking at the themes of sex and gender, this bawdy anti-war sex comedy, of which I found rather amusing, was first staged in 411 BCE. In simplistic terms, the play is the account of one woman's extraordinary mission to end the Peloponnesian War, as Lysistrata convinces the women of Greece to withhold sexual privileges from their husbands as a means of forcing the men to negotiate a peace. In other words its like a sex strike! Lysistrata, a strong minded Athenian with a great sense of individual</span>
  <span id="freeText2734548742452058562" style="display:none">Looking at the themes of sex and gender, this bawdy anti-war sex comedy, of which I found rather amusing, was first staged in 411 BCE. In simplistic terms, the play is the account of one woman's extraordinary mission to end the Peloponnesian War, as Lysistrata convinces the women of Greece to withhold sexual privileges from their husbands as a means of forcing the men to negotiate a peace. In other words its like a sex strike! Lysistrata, a strong minded Athenian with a great sense of individual responsibility, reveals her plan to take matters into her own hands and end the interminable war between Athens and Sparta. She has convened a meeting of women from various city states in Greece and, with support from the Spartan Lampito, she explains to the other women her plan, which Leads to some really laughable moments.<br /><br />Modern adaptations of the play are often performed with a feminist and/or pacifist viewpoint, but the original all the way back then was neither particularly feminist nor unreservedly pacifist. Even while apparently demonstrating empathy with the female condition, Aristophanes still tended to reinforce sexual stereotyping of women as irrational creatures in need of protection from themselves and from others. Certainly, it seems clear that Aristophanes was not actually advocating real political power for women.<br /><br />Lysistrata herself, though, is clearly an exceptional woman and, even when the other women waver in their resolution, she remains strong and committed. She is usually quite separate from the other women: she does not herself exhibit any sexual desire, has no obvious lovers or husband and does not purposely flirt with men; she is smarter, wittier and generally adopts a more serious tone than the other women. the humour throughout is highly topical and the playwright expected his audience to be familiar with myriad local personalities, places and issues, a difficulty faced by any producer trying to stage Lysistrata for modern audiences. As well as the slapstick comedy and the raucous and risqué double-entendres, much of the humour in the play derives from the audience’s knowledge of specific figures from Athens’ public life and recent history.<br /><br />Known as the 'Father of Comedy', this was great fun, and I just can't quite believe its as old as it is. I imagine it will never feel dated, long live Aristophanes! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2734548742452058562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_459941214" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/459941214" />
    <a title="Leajk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11000479-leajk"><img alt="Leajk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1344589544p2/11000479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/459941214?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leajk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leajk" href="/user/show/11000479-leajk">Leajk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486282251" href="/book/show/440998.Lysistrata">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">men, women, war monglers, pacifists</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11000479-leajk?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11000479-leajk?shelf=antiquity">antiquity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11000479-leajk?shelf=play-drama">play-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer459941214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer175502231940187115">In the introductory note in my edition a Mr. Crofts mentions that the play "<i>is notorious for its racy, almost pornographic humor</i>". I'd say that this seems to be a bit of an overstatement. <br/><br/>Surely it is not that much more racy than say a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare' title='William Shakespeare' rel='nofollow noopener'>William Shakespeare</a> play or for that matter <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93101.The_Arabian_Nights_Tales_from_a_Thousand_and_One_Nights' title='The Arabian Nights Tales from a Thousand and One Nights by Anonymous' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Arabian Nights: Tales from a Thousand and One Nights</a>? It is really all talk and no action. Surely we as modern readers can handle that? (And would anyone living in 1994, the date of this edition, really consider this</span>
  <span id="freeText175502231940187115" style="display:none">In the introductory note in my edition a Mr. Crofts mentions that the play "<i>is notorious for its racy, almost pornographic humor</i>". I'd say that this seems to be a bit of an overstatement. <br /><br />Surely it is not that much more racy than say a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/947.William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare" rel="nofollow noopener">William Shakespeare</a> play or for that matter <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93101.The_Arabian_Nights_Tales_from_a_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="The Arabian Nights Tales from a Thousand and One Nights by Anonymous" rel="nofollow noopener">The Arabian Nights: Tales from a Thousand and One Nights</a>? It is really all talk and no action. Surely we as modern readers can handle that? (And would anyone living in 1994, the date of this edition, really consider this pornographic?)<br /><br />As for the drawings of Aubrey Beardsley (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Lysistrata.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">NSFW link</a>), though they are lovely, they are also a bit... besides the point? I mean there's not actually any enormous penises running around in the play. At the most there's some naked wrinkly old men (ok, all men deciding to never read this play at this point, come back! It is a really fun play, you learn about Greek history and feel smart, yey!). <br /><br />I'm not saying that there's not a fair amount of sexual innuendo in the play, how much seems to depend a bit on which translation you have. The Beardsley drawings are clearly a rather unsubtle hint on someone being a <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[cock- <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>tease. It's just that... there so many other cool things going on as well. <br /><br />The feminist-conspiracy-theorist part of my brain starts to wonder if all this talk about <b>“oh, lordy lord women mentioning sex” </b> is simply there to distract me from what the women are actually talking about: taking the power away from men. <br /><br />Not only taking away the power, but claiming to be better at using is than the men. In this rendition the women are super clever pacifists, who'd make sure there be no war. <br /><br />To be fair, women are not portrayed as through and through good. Lysistrata berate the other women throughout the play as being lazy drunkards who can't give up sex even for even a week. She starts of by saying that women will gather for festivities celebrating Bacchus (Dionysus), Pan or Aphrodite (which the translator notes illustrates their taste for “<i>wanton pleasures</i>”), but not for discussing serious matters. She then sees her neighbour and this happens: <br /><br /><blockquote>“Lysistrata: Oh, Calonicé, my heart is on fire; I blush for our sex. Men will have it we are tricky and sly...<br /><br />Calonicé: And they are quite right, upon my word!<br /><br />Lysistrata: Yet, look you, when the women are summoned to meet for a matter of the last importance, they lie abed instead of coming.<br /><br />Calonicé: Oh, they will come, my dear; but 'tis not easy you know, for a woman to leave the house. One is busy pottering about her husband; another is getting the servant up; a third is putting her child asleep or washing the brat or feeding it.”</blockquote><br /><br />This! This is exactly what feminists keep talking about, the difficulty of uniting women for a cause, the difficulty of combining sisterhood with family, and this was written around 2400 years ago! <br /><br />When the women from near and afar are finally gathered and Lystratia lays forward her plan about depriving men of sex, the women instantly turn around and start to walk away. <b>Give up sex? What nonsense! </b> It is only after some persistent persuasion that Lysistrata manages the women to concur with her plan. The women lock themselves in the treasury of Athen and the old men of the town, and eventually the magistrate, gather to persuade them differently. <br /><br />The perhaps best part is when one of the husbands arrives sex-crazed and sex-starved from a house in disarray to implore his wife to return back home. This scene gives a whole new dimension to the word tease.<br /><br />Although the women of this book might have taken the moral high-ground, opposing war, it's obvious that Aristophanes uses the women as a group to represent the opposition that questions the status-quo, rather than saying than saying that all women are perfect (plus it doesn't hurt that it makes a good foundation for some good-ol' sex jokes).<br /><br />There are some real gems in here about pacifism, feminism and gender roles: <br /><br /><blockquote>“Lysistrata: To seize the treasury; no more money, no more war.”<br /><br />then<br /><br />“Magistrate: What do you propose to do then, pray?<br /><br />Lysistrata: You ask me that! Why, we propose to administer the treasury ourselves<br /><br />Magistrate: You do?<br /><br />Lysistrata: What is there in that a surprise to you? Do we not administer the budget of household expenses?<br /><br />Magistrate: But that is not the same thing.<br /><br />Lysistrata: How so – not the same thing?<br /><br />Magistrate: It is the treasury supplies the expenses of the War.<br /><br />Lysistrata: That's our first principle – no War!”<br /><br />or<br /><br />“Magistrate: May I die a thousand deaths ere I obey one who wears a veil!<br /><br />Lysistrata: If that's all that troubles you, here take my veil, wrap it round your head, and hold your tounge. Then take this basket; put on a girdle, card wool, munch beans. The War shall be women's business.<br /><br />Chorus of women: […] Oh! my good, gallant Lysistrata, and all my friends, be ever like a bundle of nettles; never let you anger slacken; the wind of fortune blown our way.”<br /><br />or<br /><br />“Chorus of old men: How true the saying: 'Tis impossible to live with the baggages, impossible to live without 'em.”<br /><br />or<br /><br />“Chorus of old men: If we give them the least hold over us, 'tis all up! their audacity will know no bounds! We shall see them building ships, and fighting sea-fights like Artemisia*; nay if they want to mount and ride as cavalry, we had best cashier the knights, for indeed women excel in riding, and have a fine, firm seat for the gallop. Just think of all those squadrons of Amazons Micon has painted for us engaged in hand-to-hand combat with men.”<br /><br />(*a queen who fought alongside with the Persian king Xerxes at sea)<br /><br />or<br /><br />“Lysistrata: Silence then! Now - 'Whenas the swallows, fleeing before the hoopoes, shall have all flocked together in one place, and shall refrain them from all amorous commerce, then will be the end of all the ills of life; yea, and Zeus, which doth thunder in the skies, shall set above what was once below...<br /><br />Chorus of women: What! shall the men be underneath?<br /><br />But if dissension do arise among the swallows, and they take wing from the holy Temple, 'twill be said there is never more wanton bird in all the world.'”</blockquote><br /><br />Again this excellent theme of the importance of women's unity. Sisterhood. Ah, how wonderful. <br /><br />Oh, and of course we really like our men too. We don't actually want to take away all of you power, we'd just like to share it equally and discuss a thing our two. Like this thing with war...</span>
  <a data-text-id="175502231940187115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1243257248" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1243257248" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7022024-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470679918p2/7022024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1243257248?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/7022024-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6241622-lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7022024-mike?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1243257248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3451266307255745880">I wouldn't be surprised if Lysistrata was the first sex comedy (that's a genre, right?). Sex (or lack there of ) drives the plot and innuendos abound:<br/><br/><b>Lysistrata</b>: But I tell you, here's a far more weighty object.<br/><b>Calonice</b>: What is it all about, dear Lysistrata, that you've called the women hither in a troop? What kind of object is it?<br/><b>Lysistrata</b>: A tremendous one!<br/><b>Calonice</b>: And long?<br/><b>Lysistrata</b>: Indeed, it may be very lengthy.<br/><b>Calonice</b>: Then why aren't they here?<br/><b>Lysistrata</b>: No Man's connected with it;</span>
  <span id="freeText3451266307255745880" style="display:none">I wouldn't be surprised if Lysistrata was the first sex comedy (that's a genre, right?). Sex (or lack there of ) drives the plot and innuendos abound:<br /><br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: But I tell you, here's a far more weighty object.<br /><b>Calonice</b>: What is it all about, dear Lysistrata, that you've called the women hither in a troop? What kind of object is it?<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: A tremendous one!<br /><b>Calonice</b>: And long?<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: Indeed, it may be very lengthy.<br /><b>Calonice</b>: Then why aren't they here?<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: No Man's connected with it; if that was the case, they'd soon come fluttering along. No, no. It concerns an object I've felt over and turned this way and that for sleepless nights.<br /><b>Calonice</b>: I must be fine to stand such long attention.<br /><br />On its surface this play is about the women of Greece withholding sex from the men to force them to make peace during the destructive Peloponnesian War. In truth I am pretty sure Aristophanes just wanted an excuse to make as many sexual innuendos and gender stereotypes as possibly.<br /><br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: We must refrain from every depth of love... Why do you turn your backs? Where are you going? Why do you bite your lips and shake your heads? Why are your faces blanched? Why do you weep? Will you or won't you [join me in the sex strike]...<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: No I won't. Let the war proceed.<br /><br />~~~<br /><br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Don't go, please don't go, Myrrhine [his wife and a sex striker]. At least hear our child... don't you feel pity for the child? He's not been fed or washed now for six days.<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: I certainly pity him with so heartless a father...<br />...<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: You love me! Then dear girl, let me also love you.<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: You must be joking. The boy's looking on.<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Here, Manes, take the child home! ... There, he's gone. There's nothing in the way now. <br /><br />The women fret about their homes going to ruin while they are away. One even tries to fake a pregnancy:<br /><br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: What nonsense is this?<br /><b>Woman</b>: I'll drop any minute<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: Yesterday you weren't with child.<br /><b>Woman</b>: But I am today. O let me find a midwife Lysistrata. O Quickly!<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: Now what story is this you tell? What is this hard lump here?<br /><b>Woman</b>: It's a male child.<br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: By Aphrodite, it isn't. Your belly's hollow, and it has the feel of metal... Well, I soon can see. You hussy, it's Athene's scared helm, and you said you were with child.<br /><br />Some, however, are pretty damn good at messing with the mind's of their men:<br /> <br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: But how can I break my oath? <br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Leave that to me, I'll take all the risk<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: Well, I'll make you comfortable<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Don't worry. I'd as soon lie on the grass<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: No, by Apollo, in spite of all your faults I won't have you lying on the nasty earth... Rest here on the bench, while I arrange my clothes. O what a nuisance, I must find some cushions first.<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Why some cushions? Please don't get them!<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: What? Plain, hard wood? Never, by Artemis! That would be too vulgar<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: Open your arms!<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>:...Here the cushions are. Lie down while I - O dear! but what a shame, you need more pillows.<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: I don't want them dear.<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: But I do... Why, you've no blanket.<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: It's not the silly blanket's warmth but yours I want.<br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: Never mind. You'll soon have both. I'll come right back... Would you like me to perfume you?<br /><b>Cinesias</b>: By Apollo, no! <br /><b>Myrrhine</b>: By Aphrodite, I'll do it anyway!<br /><br />etc etc etc <br /><br />(Sufficed to say, the oath was not broken, though I think the poor man's will was)<br /><br />Aristophanes also takes plenty of opportunities to insert sexual innuendo because he can:<br /><br />[after peace has been agreed upon]<br /><b>Athenians</b>: I want to strip at once and plow my land.<br /><b>Spartans</b>: And mine I want to fertilize at once.<br /><br />~~~<br /><br /><b>Men's chorus</b>: We must take a stand and keep to it, for if we yield the smallest bit to their importunity then nowhere from their inroads will be left to us immunity...And if they mount, the Knights they'll rob of a job, <b> for everyone knows how talented they all are in the saddle, having long practiced how to straddle</b>...<br /><br />In spite of all the sex and joking, the play does have a few good messages:<br /><br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: You [men] wrack hellenic cities, bloody Hellas with deaths of her own sons, while yonder clangs the gathering menace of barbarians.<br /><br />~~~<br /><br /><b>Lysistrata</b>: It should not prejudice my voice that I'm not born a man, if I say something advantageous to the present situation. For I'm taxed too, and as a toll provide men for the nation while, miserable greybeards... contribute nothing of any importance whatever to our needs.<br /><br />But mostly this was a play about sex and sex jokes that were shockingly modern in their convention (might have just been the translation). This was a quick and enjoyable read, just remember this was meant to be a bit of an absurdist satire so don't take the actions and decisions of the characters very seriously. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3451266307255745880" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1913302134" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1913302134" />
    <a title="Ana " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5237420-ana"><img alt="Ana " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1573472177p2/5237420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1913302134?book_show_action=true">Feb 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ana " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ana " href="/user/show/5237420-ana">Ana </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780146001666" href="/book/show/1133571.Lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=girls-kick-ass">girls-kick-ass</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=humour-me">humour-me</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1913302134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6377398010590650247">Why do we live under the impression that the Greeks were such serious philosophers, when one of their favorite past-time was listening to dick jokes? I loved this, I do enjoy the occasional dick joke. One has to read this and play it in one's mind at the same time...</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7085248831903012589">To stop the Peloponnesian War, the namesake heroin Lysistrata talks all the warrior's wives into going on a sex strike. This certainly is one of the earliest examples (to remain) of a play staging such an event. However, this is not the only comedy written by Aristophanes on the many issues raised by the cohabitation of men and women, far from it!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2000501431" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2000501431" />
    <a title="david" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67794375-david"><img alt="david" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597156400p2/67794375.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2000501431?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="david" class="user" itemprop="url" name="david" href="/user/show/67794375-david">david</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34736257-lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/67794375-david?shelf=plays-drama">plays-drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2000501431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7714906326471003158">In the beginning, G-d created the heavens and the earth.<br/><br/>A short time thereafter, he created Adam, and from Adam, Eve.<br/><br/>Then there was nature.<br/><br/>Then some decorating.<br/><br/>It was then time to enact society, of which, it was decided by G-d, would be left to the people wandering around.<br/><br/>A Civilization was forming…<br/><br/>Soon after that first very hectic week, a philosopher appeared. What was unusual about him was his decidedly German accent, thousands of years before there was a Frankfurter.<br/><br/>But philosophy can be dry,</span>
  <span id="freeText7714906326471003158" style="display:none">In the beginning, G-d created the heavens and the earth.<br /><br />A short time thereafter, he created Adam, and from Adam, Eve.<br /><br />Then there was nature.<br /><br />Then some decorating.<br /><br />It was then time to enact society, of which, it was decided by G-d, would be left to the people wandering around.<br /><br />A Civilization was forming…<br /><br />Soon after that first very hectic week, a philosopher appeared. What was unusual about him was his decidedly German accent, thousands of years before there was a Frankfurter.<br /><br />But philosophy can be dry, even by Bedouin standards.<br /><br />Next, the ground one day roared and with a great swell, rose a comic.<br /><br />The comedienne, a female naturally, was strolling around Eden when she bumped into Adam.<br /> <br />She eyed this unusual creature, head to toe, and asked, “How did you do that?”<br /><br />He looked down on his naked body and said, “Ah, it’s nothing.”<br /><br />“I know,” she said, “but I must use it in my act. Where can I get one?”<br /><br />Adam shrugged (pre-Atlas).<br /><br />“I’ll just use yours,” said the determined comedienne, and she yanked and yanked but it would not come off.<br /><br />The philosopher studied the interaction and ran behind a giant banyan tree and began to excogitate.<br /><br />Within a month, the first high-Priest was seen passing out figs to some three-hundred-year-old children, or young people. “Only eat organic,” was his religious dogma. “And those leaves over there, the ones that numb your gums, chew in moderation.”<br /> <br />Not a week after, a psychologist was created to listen to the complaints that had begun amid earth’s increasing inhabitants. The recurring discussion that was heard from the patients in a quiet surrounding of bushes and dirt, was Smell.<br /> <br />Even with hundreds of hours of discussion, everything remained stirred and stinky, until one day Ug came upon a sea. Ug drowned quickly as he investigated it, but afterwards, everyone bathed there, careful not to recreate the accident that Ug had.<br /> <br />The psychologist had more time to discuss inbreeding (but I love my nephew), a very topical topic. And also, boredom. It was the same thing every day. “We have nothing to do.” The psychologist would frequently suggest playing with the smaller critters to her patients, but many were maimed and her patient list was not large.<br /><br />There were several highly intelligent monkeys swinging through the trees. They were stopped and silenced, with bananas, by some of the elders (the ancients were, like, one thousand years old in those days).<br /><br />“We are in need of three apes. One to become a magistrate and two more to act in the interests of those who are unfortunate. We will call you barristers. I must first warn you that all conflicts will be resolved pro bono because currency has not been invented yet. Do I have any volunteers?”<br /> <br />Say what you will about simians, but they are not dummies. They shrieked for hours and then for days. No compensation, no inflated bills, no churning, no retainers, no unnecessary depositions? So, they brought in the disinterested philosopher as arbiter, because there was suddenly no interest. He chose a magistrate, two advocates, and a billing/ accounts receivable monkey. Hypocrisy was learned by the four and agreed on between them as the a priori rule to apply to their lives and those they will subjugate.<br /><br />Then came all the other elements of society we are all too familiar with. And with each new important person, G-d seemed to slip into the background. But maybe he was watching all the while.<br /><br />Anyway, what does this have to do with Lysistrata? Not sure. Sometimes I key in stuff after mixing serotonin inhibitors just for fun. Let me think. Yeah. This guy, Aristophanes, was maybe the first Neil Simon or Oscar Wilde before they existed. He wrote these comedic plays a long time ago, maybe around 400 BCE. While wearing a tunic, which was in style then for both genders. Easy to wash and easy to fold and a little bit racy, if you are into the retro thing.<br /><br />So, Stoph, as we called this playwright in those days, existed during the Peloponnesian Wars. The Spartans and the Athenians. They loved to fight. It seems like a very human wont. Lysistrata was a hot woman, even more persuasive with her flimsy tunic. Add to that brains, and the ability to motivate others, and affect change, and we have perhaps our first anti-war sit in.<br /> <br />She convinced all the women of a certain age to hang with her at the Acropolis and to lock the doors behind them. No men allowed. Think Chippendales without the Chippens.<br /> <br />She believed that depriving men of sex would induce them to cease warring. And that is all I am going to share because the topic is too disturbing for me to contemplate.<br /><br />I love this stuff. Is there really anybody out there that believes that we ‘moderns’ are different from our ancestors?<br /> <br />This play can be read in one sitting. Quite funny.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7714906326471003158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_887545018" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/887545018" />
    <a title="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat"><img alt="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397575578p2/23003918.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/887545018?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜" href="/user/show/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat">Erin the Avid Reader ⚜BFF&#39;s with the Cheshire Cat⚜</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780941051026" href="/book/show/1570.Lysistrata">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=reading-the-world">reading-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=my-book-reviews">my-book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=the-human-brain-and-body">the-human-brain-and-body</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=humor-and-comedy">humor-and-comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=adult">adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=greco-roman">greco-roman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23003918-erin-the-avid-reader-bff-s-with-the-cheshire-cat?shelf=plays-and-screenplays">plays-and-screenplays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer887545018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11024162396345747731">As you can see, there are no highlighted stars for this review. The reason for this is not because I loathed the play, but simply because I have read three different translations of Lysistrata, each unique in translation. If you read what appears to be a bad translation of the play, then that is not the fault of Aristophanes, but of the translator(s). With that being said, instead of one rating to finalize it, I am posting three ratings and reviews, one for each translation I have read; from the</span>
  <span id="freeText11024162396345747731" style="display:none">As you can see, there are no highlighted stars for this review. The reason for this is not because I loathed the play, but simply because I have read three different translations of Lysistrata, each unique in translation. If you read what appears to be a bad translation of the play, then that is not the fault of Aristophanes, but of the translator(s). With that being said, instead of one rating to finalize it, I am posting three ratings and reviews, one for each translation I have read; from the best to the worst. I will link the source of the play for each one.<br><br>Before I do, I'll just say really quickly what I think of the play itself: When translated correctly, it's really, really funny and engaging. I'll elaborate more on it when I talk about the first translation.<br><br>With that being said, let's get Aristophane'd (or UN-Aristophane'd for one translation)! <br><br>THE FIRST TRANSLATION:<br>Aristophanes: The Complete Plays<br>Translated by Paul Roche<br>Published by New American Library<br>Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/B001NQGN56/ref=dp-kindle-redirect?_encoding=UTF8&amp;btkr=1">https://www.amazon.com/dp/B001NQGN56/...</a><br><br>RATING: 4.5 STARS<br><br>From what I've heard on a taped lecture from a Brown University professor who specialized in Classic Greek lit and language, this book (to what he perceived) contains the closest translation we can (or just have) gotten to Aristophanes' original play. When translating a work, you're not only translating words, but also figuring out what they could have meant back then and how they were delivered. FOR EXAMPLE: If somehow over thousands of years the English language disappeared and people found remains of an American play, they might translate the word "rain" wrong, as we also have words pronounced is similar ways, such as reign and rein. All three of these words sound the same but have different meanings.<br><br>From what I've heard, Roche translated all of Aristophanes works (including Lysistrata) in a very articulate way. Reading the play from this book myself, I think I can agree with this professor.<br><br>This is probably the best and most faithful adaptation of Lysistrata that exists right now...but what does that also mean? It also means it most likely the crudest one that exists at this point, but that's what also makes it better; for the original play written in Ancient Greek was very crude and did not shy away from sexual matters and foul language. This translation has a lot of sexual banter, some f-bombs, sexual slang, etc. It's also a really, really well written play that has, when of course translated well like this version, wonderful chemistry and at times stream-of-conscious like dialogue. I can credit this translation as well for it's use of footnotes when something obscure is referenced. Very useful, indeed!<br><br>I found this version to have the best take on Lysistrata, herself. I loved how strong-willed and confident she was instead of being more lenient and...well, I'll get to that translation in a little bit. <br><br>I've read this version three times and it never gets old. In fact, I think it gets a bit better with every viewing (there is some word play and references you don't always catch the first time). They got the names of the characters right and they never reference anything outside of that time period (you'll see what I fully mean by this , later).<br><br>Overall? This version is an exquisite example of a superb translation that is close to the original work of Aristophanes. Bravo, Paul Roche! You deserve an M&amp;M cookie.<br><br>SECOND TRANSLATION:<br>Lysistrata (Hackett Classics)<br>Translated by Sarah Ruden<br>Published by Hackett Publishing Co.<br>Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.fishpond.com/Books/Lysistrata-Hackett-Classics-Aristophanes-Sarah-Ruden-Translated-by/9780872206045">http://www.fishpond.com/Books/Lysistr...</a><br><br>FINAL RATING: 3.75 STARS<br><br>I just want to let you know people, these are simply my opinions with SOME factual basis. I know some people absolutely love this translation of the play; and to it's credit, the translator of course does have some backup. She has a PhD in Classics and has generated some very positive reviews about her works. This is simply my opinion of the translation. No hate comments needed.<br><br>I just gotta ask though...who drew the cover for this book? I don't want to sound mean, but it's just repugnant to look at. It creeps me out for some reason. I just wanted to get that out of the way...and I'm not rating the books based off that.<br><br>As I was going to say, I really don't find this translation of Lysistrata that bad at all. In many ways it's quite like Roche's translation and keeps the names of the characters similar and their motives the same. So then why the lower rating?<br><br>I think I rated this one lower because of how I noticed Sarah Ruden tried to make the dialogue much shorter than it had been. In some parts, instead of long, Shakespearean-like prose, verse, and rhythm, some of those parts have been trimmed down to a mere couple sentences. Maybe Ruden assumed that a lot of the poetry Aristophanes used in this play was pointless to the plot. If this is the case, then I wholeheartedly disagree. If it isn't, I still don't know why a lot of it is trimmed down. This would be okay for a beginner but if you're studying for a class and assigned the play, then I'm not sure this would be the right translation for you.<br><br>I'm going to be honest, I thought it was boring for what it was trying to adapt. Lysistrata is supposed to be dramatic, crass, hilarious, and in many ways, dogmatic. This translation though is...satisfactory. Yes it is still engaging, but it lacks the substance that Roche's translation had. Again, maybe Ruden thought the play should be taken on a more serious note instead of a comedic one. It depends on the perception of the translator and the reader. In my opinion, Lysistrata is comedy while hilarious and just plain weird at times, it's also preaching the fact that women in Greece are mistreated, with the play itself using irony and clever word play to preach to it's audience. In many ways you could even consider Lysistrata a feminist play (even though feminism wasn't a concept then--at least, that we know of).<br><br>Overall, this adaption is alright. I read through it pretty quickly and still have it on my bookshelf. Maybe I'll re-read it again, soon and see if I missed anything. <br><br>THIRD TRANSLATION<br>Lysistrata (Focus Classics Library)<br>Translated by Jeffrey Henderson<br>Published by Focus<br>Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.amazon.com/dp/B00PRHFW0Y/ref=dp-kindle-redirect?_encoding=UTF8&amp;btkr=1">https://www.amazon.com/dp/B00PRHFW0Y/...</a><br><br>FINAL RATING: 1 STAR<br><br>Aye...<br><br><a href="http://photobucket.com/images/wtf%20gif" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1476208764ra/20813876.gif" alt="wtf gif photo: WTF tumblr_m4dlendutH1qedebno4_r1_250.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>I've been foreshadowing this translation too much in this review.<br><br>This translation is really bizarre. I mean it. It's...very, very strange in a horrible way. I'd really love to sit down and have a serious talk with the translator.<br><br>I really hate to start using GIF's in a serious review, but in this scenario, I just need them here to express how I felt about this translation. <br><br>Oh boy, where do I begin?<br><br>Okay, I'll start off by saying the names are all correct. There. Done with the pro.<br><br>Now its the cons.<br><br>First off, there are references to things hundreds if not thousands of years after the year this play was written and during the years it took place (around 431-404 BCE). In the intro of this edition, the translator speaks about modernizing parts of it. Alright, that actually sounds really interesting! A version of Lysistrata taking place in the 20th or 21st century? Why not?<br><br>That's when this translation got...weird.<br><br>Calonice, one of the women who finally volunteers to join the sex strike, speaks about beforehand she would have rather burned her best pair of designer jeans. Then Lysistrata before this references women wearing high heels. Alright. It's supposed to be a modern adaptation, right? Well, I thought so...until they all reference that they're husbands are fighting in Sparta and want them back in Athens.<br><br>So...what the hell is this supposed to be? Does this take place in 20th-century America or in the 5th century BCE during the Peloponnesian war?? They didn't have Old Navy back then and high heels were not even invented until the 14th century or later in Naples. Why are these things being talked about if it takes place in the 5th century! I'm assuming it's the 5th century during the war, as in the beginning of the play they show a map of where the war was occurring in 421 BCE. I think my brain just fizzled a little bit. <br><br>There's even a part where a Spartan soldier references Freud. <br><br><a href="http://photobucket.com/images/what%20gif" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380367192ra/719632.gif" alt="what gif photo: What tumblr_ln7cx2XL6i1qfcb7s.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>WHAT IS THIS!?<br><br>As for just the translation if you ignore all this modern BS in a 5th century society? I'll tell you what it is. Repetitive and dull without a substance. That sounds very harsh, but it's my opinion and I'm sticking to it. Also the dialogue seemed very clunky in this version. There were parts I'd have to re-read five times an exchange of sentences between characters because I'd have no idea what the heck they were even talking about or why they were taking about it! <br><br>I somehow ended up finishing this, but it took much longer than it did the other two. This probably isn't the worst translation of Lysistrata out there but it's gotta be down there. At least the Spartan's don't have annoying Scottish accents like Jack Lindsay's translation or Parker's translation where they sound like hillbillies (because that makes a lot of sense, right)!? You just have to learn to identify a good translation and a bad one. I hope this review is helpful for some. Again, it is my opinion, but I do stand by these opinions strongly. <br><br>Also no, I am not reviewing Parker's and Lindsay's translations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11024162396345747731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
